            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/aenm.202404685')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd2beb551-008a-44f7-b887-e1a18db77dea'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d159978110" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Although layered vanadium oxides are extensively studied for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), their broader application is hindered by the instability of their bulk-phase structure, sluggish electron/ion-transfer kinetics, and insufficient active sites. Herein, a novel synergistic strategy is proposed to modulate the electronic structure of VO by incorporating Na<sup>+</sup> cations into deep V─O layers (D-NVO<sub>x</sub>), resulting in significant structural modifications such as enlarged lattice spacing, the generation of abundant oxygen vacancies, and disordering of the V─O layers. Comprehensive analytical characterizations and theoretical calculations reveal that this synergy induces reversible Na<sup>+</sup> pseudocapacitive intercalation and reconstructs low-energy barrier channels, thereby accelerating Na-ion diffusion kinetics. Moreover, the oxygen vacancies dramatically boost electronic conductivity and reinforce structural stability. The reduced crystallinity and lattice distortion result in dense nanointerfaces, potentially widening diffusion channels and providing additional active sites for fast surface Na-ion storage. Owing to these merits, the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode achieves a high specific capacity (505 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>‒1</sup>), outstanding cyclic stability (96% capacity retention over 2000 cycles at 2.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>), and superior rate performance (280 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 5.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>). This work provides in-depth insights into enhancing electrochemical energy storage in SIBs by modulating electronic structure and structural defects.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202404685-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202404685-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the rapid global transition toward clean energy and the pursuit of a carbon-neutral economy, there is a critical demand for large-scale energy storage systems with high energy density and superior cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0001_R_d159978101e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0002_R_d159978101e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - ACS Energy Lett.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have gained significant attention due to the sustainability of sodium resources, cost-efficiency, and their similar working principles to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0003_R_d159978101e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0004_R_d159978101e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the insertion of large sodium ions into the graphite anodes commonly used in LIBs is thermodynamically unfavorable, hindering the achievement of high performance in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0005_R_d159978101e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0006_R_d159978101e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Environ. Sci.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the development of anode materials is hampered by poor reversible capacity, cycling stability, and rate performance due to sluggish kinetics and significant volume changes during repeated sodiation and desodiation cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0007_R_d159978101e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0008_R_d159978101e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Inf. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, developing suitable anode materials that provide reversible and stable Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage is vital for enhanced SIB performance.</p>
         
         <p>Among the various anode candidates, vanadium-based oxides (VOs) have emerged as highly desirable electrode materials due to their potential advantages, such as large theoretical capacity, multiple electron redox reactions, and cost-effectiveness.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0009_R_d159978101e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0010_R_d159978101e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite these advancements, their narrow interlayer spacing, poor ionic/electronic conductivity, and insufficient active sites can cause notable volume expansion, sluggish kinetics, and structural instability, resulting in significant capacity decline.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0011_R_d159978101e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A">11</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0012_R_d159978101e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To overcome these limitations, various strategies have been adopted, including morphology and size control,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0011_R_d159978101e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A">11</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0013_R_d159978101e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> surface modification (CNT, graphene, etc),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0014_R_d159978101e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">14</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0015_R_d159978101e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> heteroatom doping,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0016_R_d159978101e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and interlayer engineering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0017_R_d159978101e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among these approaches, interlayer engineering has proven effective in tuning the intrinsic properties of VOs by reinforcing interlayer interactions. Typically, this involves repeating a certain synthesis method several times to introduce diverse cations (e.g., Li<sup>+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, Mn<sup>+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Cu<sup>2+</sup>, Al<sup>3+</sup>, and NH<sup>4+</sup>) with different radii and doping amounts, which serve as pillars in the layered frameworks and expanding the interlayer spacing to facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0018_R_d159978101e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0019_R_d159978101e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the effect of dual-ion<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0020_R_d159978101e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Energy">20</a>-<a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0023_R_d159978101e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and multiple-ion intercalation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0024_R_d159978101e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> along with the incorporation of conductive polymers (e.g., PEDOT, PANI, BTA, and C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0025_R_d159978101e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Mater.">25</a>-<a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0028_R_d159978101e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been extensively investigated, leading to pronounced enhancement of the electrochemical kinetics. However, most intercalators fail to penetrate into deeper layers of VO, leading to irreversible phase transitions similar to their bulk counterparts, thus severely impeding their electrochemical performance. Additionally, the issue of insufficient sodium storage sites remains unresolved, implying their restricted modulation.</p>
         
         <p>Well-designed crystal defects have shown considerable advantages in altering the surface chemistry and electronic structure of transition metal oxide, consequently exposing abundant active sites, and narrowing the band gaps, thereby improving sodium storage and rate capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0029_R_d159978101e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Mater. Chem. A">29</a>-<a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0031_R_d159978101e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, generating defects is often associated with structural disorder, which is crucial for enabling rapid pseudocapacitive multi-electron reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0032_R_d159978101e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0033_R_d159978101e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Environ. Sci.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, a comprehensive engineering solution that integrates all these benefits remains elusive, which limits their further development and practical application in SIBs. Therefore, developing an effective synergistic engineering approach to simultaneously address the narrow interlayer spacing, irreversible phase transitions, and electrical transport issues of vanadium oxides is vital for achieving anodes with both fast and stable performance from the surface to the deeper layers.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we introduce a high-performance VO-based anode, engineered through an advanced deep pillaring treatment (donated as D-NVO<sub>x</sub>). This novel approach induces critical structural enhancements, including enlarged lattice spacing, abundant oxygen vacancies, partial amorphization, and rich nanointerfaces. The Na<sup>+</sup> pillaring within the deep V─O layers is accomplished via a straightforward low-temperature hydrothermal method, imparting unique properties not observed in bulk VO or surface/shallow-pillared layered metal oxides. The well-designed D-NVO<sub>x</sub> anode enables reversible and rapid pseudocapacitive multi-electron reactions. Comprehensive analytical characterizations and theoretical calculations reveal that the Na<sup>+</sup> pillars and oxygen vacancies alter the Na-ion transfer pathways in the bulk VO and significantly reduce the energy barrier. Simultaneously, the expanded interlayer spacing increases the active surface area and improves electrolyte penetration. The reduced crystallinity and lattice distortion generate abundant nanointerfaces, which provide additional active sites, facilitate fast Na-ion migration, and reinforce structural stability (<b>Scheme</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0007">1</a>). Thanks to its structural advantages, the tailored D-NVO<sub>x</sub> anode achieved a high reversible specific capacity of 505 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>‒1</sup> and retained 96% of its initial capacity over 2000 cycles at 2.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>. Even at the high rate of 5 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, it still presented a high capacity of 280 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>, outperforming most transition metal oxide electrodes. This strategy not only overcomes intrinsic limitations in kinetics and stability in non and pillared layered electrodes but also guides the structural and electronic regulation of layered transition metal oxides toward high-performance SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202404685-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/33e10e8a-9f28-4ffa-86f9-01e25ba731ef/aenm202404685-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/33e10e8a-9f28-4ffa-86f9-01e25ba731ef/aenm202404685-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fd6b1dd4-adab-4275-ac67-913490803e51/aenm202404685-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/33e10e8a-9f28-4ffa-86f9-01e25ba731ef/aenm202404685-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202404685-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202404685" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Illustration of deep layer pillaring in vanadium oxides: l) Bulk vanadium oxide (VO); red, transparent red, blue, and yellow atoms represent oxygen, oxygen vacancies, vanadium, and sodium, respectively. ll) Surface Pillaring: Sodium ions intercalate into the surface layer of VO, causing expansion while maintaining the VO structure. lll) Deep Pillaring: Sodium ions intercalate deeper, expanding the interlayer spacing, generating oxygen vacancies, and disordering the VO structure. This results in the formation of a high density of nanointerfaces and enriched active sites, which are crucial for reversible and fast Na⁺ migrations, thereby enhancing electrochemical performance.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202404685-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202404685-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussions</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202404685-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202404685-sec-0030-title">2.1 Characterizations of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> Nanosheets</h3>
            
            <p>The fabrication process of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> is schematically shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0001">1a</a>. Briefly, VO(acac)<sub>2</sub> is reduced in a dilute aqueous formaldehyde solution without any surfactant, and a precise amount of CH<sub>3</sub>COONa is further added as a sodium source. CH<sub>3</sub>COONa was intentionally chosen rather than the more reactive sodium salts (e.g., NaOH, NaCl, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, etc.) to regulate the release of sodium ions into the deep VOOH layers. This facilitates the expansion of lattice spacing, the formation of abundant crystal defects, and the disordering of the structure, which are beneficial for improving electrical conductivity and surface Na-ion storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0030_R_d159978101e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Sci.">30</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0034_R_d159978101e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For comparison, the rich defects K<sup>+</sup> doping VO (D-KVO<sub>x</sub>), as well as defects-free vanadium oxide (VO) without ionic doping, were also synthesized as control samples. X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were conducted to characterize the crystal structure of the prepared samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0001">1b</a>). The pristine VO shows a highly crystalline structure with similar diffraction peaks of bilayered vanadium oxide phase V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>∙1.6H<sub>2</sub>O and the M<sub>x</sub>-intercalated V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (PDF #45-1363).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0023_R_d159978101e694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0035_R_d159978101e694" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Sci.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202404685-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d9c50868-2b3b-457c-8948-65bbf09b2def/aenm202404685-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d9c50868-2b3b-457c-8948-65bbf09b2def/aenm202404685-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/caad1cd0-9d8b-4845-a227-20f4a7bf7fb1/aenm202404685-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d9c50868-2b3b-457c-8948-65bbf09b2def/aenm202404685-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202404685-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202404685" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Synthetic mechanism and characterization of Na-doped VO nanosheets. a) Schematic illustration for the preparation of D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. b) XRD patterns of VO, D-KVO<sub>x</sub>, and D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. TEM and HRTEM images with SAED pattern of c,d) VO and e,f) D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. g) Atomic resolution STEM profile of D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. h) EDX elemental mapping images of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> showing the homogeneous distribution of Na, V, and O elements.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>A dominant peak is observed at 8.14° corresponding to a lattice spacing of 10.8 Å of bulk VO. Upon Na⁺ incorporation, only two broad diffraction peaks appear at 6.2° and 25.4°, corresponding to the (001) and (110) planes, respectively. The disappearance of bulk VO peaks indicates Na⁺ pillaring within the deep layers. Otherwise, all these peaks belonging to VO should be preserved.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0026_R_d159978101e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Environ. Sci.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0001">1b</a>, the interlayer spacing of VO expands significantly from 10.8 Å to 14.2 Å, confirming the successful incorporation of Na<sup>+</sup>. Furthermore, the peak at 6.2° becomes more pronounced when the Na⁺ concentration is doubled (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the intensity of the (001) peak in VO significantly decreases following the incorporation of Na<sup>+</sup>, suggesting that Na<sup>+</sup> doping promotes the formation of a deficient disordered structure. Such a structure can facilitate 3D diffusion pathways for both ions and electrolytes, consequently boosting the efficiency of electrochemical reactions. A comparable structural change was also observed in the D-KVO<sub>x</sub> sample, where K⁺ doping increased the interlayer distance of VO to 13.9 Å (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0001">1b</a>). This indicates a stronger interaction between K<sup>+</sup> and the V─O layer, which may expel water molecules from the layer, resulting in a reduced interlayer spacing for D-KVO<sub>x</sub> compared to D-NVO<sub>x</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0036_R_d159978101e764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Mater. Chem. A">36</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0037_R_d159978101e764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Small">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The molar ratio of Na to V was determined to be ≈1.04:8 by ICP-MS (inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometry) (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, thermogravimetric (TG) analysis was conducted to analyze the water loss in VO, D-KVO<sub>x</sub>, and D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), VO exhibits significantly higher mass loss than D-KVO<sub>x</sub> and D-NVO<sub>x</sub>, suggesting that the intercalated K⁺ and Na⁺ substitute structural water in VO layers. Furthermore, the reduced peak intensity of the ─OH bond in D-KVO<sub>x</sub> and D-NVO<sub>x</sub> compared to VO confirms the loss of structural H<sub>2</sub>O during the deep layer pillaring reactions.</p>
            
            <p>Various techniques were utilized to comprehensively analyze the morphology and elucidate the role of deep Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation in pristine VO layered materials. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images demonstrate that vanadium oxide (VO) exhibits a flower-like morphology comprising oriented 2D highly interconnected nanosheets (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Transmission electron microscope (TEM) observations confirm the 2D morphology, revealing numerous stacked nanosheets with a thickness of ≈35&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The formation of the flower-like morphology can be ascribed to the presence of formaldehyde in the hydrothermal synthesis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0038_R_d159978101e804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Res.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After Na⁺ doping, the SEM image shows micron-sized particles composed of uniformly dispersed crumpled nanosheets (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that Na⁺ expands the stacked VO nanosheets, forming a loose net-like morphology (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0001">1e</a>). This observation implies a transition from compact nanosheets to a more open, unconnected network. Such a homogeneous 3D nanoarchitecture can prevent agglomeration and is favorable for superior electrolyte–electrode contact and enhanced solid-state Na⁺ ion diffusion.</p>
            
            <p>To gain a deeper insight into the crystal and electronic structures, high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM), scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM), and energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) analyses have been conducted on VO layers before and after doping. The HRTEM images of pristine VO reveal a highly ordered crystallographic structure with a lattice spacing of 3.4 Å (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0001">1d</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), consistent with the XRD results. In contrast, following Na⁺ intercalation, the interlayer spacing expands to 14.2 Å, accompanied by increasing structural disorder. This is evidenced by vacancies, lattice mismatch, and partial amorphization in the HRTEM image of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0001">1f</a>). Additionally, V─O vacancy clusters are observed in multiple regions, as shown in the HAADF-STEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0001">1g</a>) and its inverse fast Fourier transform (IFFT) (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The distorted lattice structure is further confirmed by the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0001">1f</a>). This pronounced structural disorder is also apparent along the [001] axis in the D-KVO<sub>x</sub> sample (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that cation incorporation extends into the deeper V─O layers, corroborating the observed structural changes. Notably, the reduced crystallinity and lattice distortion create numerous nanointerfaces, as seen in HRTEM images, which are potentially favorable for accelerating Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. The EDS mapping images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0001">1h</a> show that the V, O, and Na elements are uniformly distributed throughout the entire loose net-like structure, proving the successful incorporation of Na⁺ into the VO lattices (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0001">1h</a>). Overall, these characteristics confirm that the incorporation of cation doping into the deep VO layers induces lattice distortions, defect formation, and the reconstruction of rich nanointerfaces, unlike shallow or surface intercalation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0020_R_d159978101e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Energy">20</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0026_R_d159978101e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Environ. Sci.">26</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0039_R_d159978101e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Energy Lett.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0002">2a</a> shows the electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) spectra of the as-prepared samples. D-NVO<sub>x</sub> exhibits a significantly stronger signal at a g-value of 2.003, indicating a higher concentration of oxygen defects after Na doping. In addition, two dominant signals at g ≈ 1.895 and 2.021 are also observed in D-NVO<sub>x</sub> but are not visible for pristine VO, suggesting the increase of vanadium vacancies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0040_R_d159978101e871" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method was used to measure the specific surface area (SSA) and pore size distribution of VO and D-NVO<sub>x</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0002">2b</a>). D-NVO<sub>x</sub> possesses a significantly higher SSA (61.67 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>‒1</sup>) compared to VO (19.52 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>‒1</sup>), and the average pore diameter is dramatically reduced after Na doping (VO: 10.7&nbsp;nm vs D-NVO<sub>x</sub>:6.8&nbsp;nm). This substantial decrease in pore size is likely due to the disruption of long-range ordered porosity caused by doping and the formation of oxygen vacancies. The enhanced surface area and mesoporosity of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> are advantageous for improving contact with electrolytes, facilitating charge diffusion kinetics, and providing additional Na-ion storage in the mesopores. Raman spectroscopy was further performed to determine the chemical bonding of VO and D-NVO<sub>x</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0002">2c</a>). The Raman profile of VO reveals eight distinct bands within the range of 40 to 1000&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, implying a highly ordered structure. After the introduction of Na<sup>+</sup>, these peaks gradually shift and broaden, providing direct evidence that VO transformed into a deficient disordered structure of D-NVO<sub>x</sub>, consistent with the EPR observations. In particular, the peaks at 94, 192, and 280&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> can be assigned to the bending vibration of V═O, while the dominant peak at 138 cm<sup>−1</sup> is ascribed to the bending vibration of ─O─V─O─V─ chains, associated with the layered structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0023_R_d159978101e913" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the O─V─O─V bending vibration is redshifted, suggesting the lattice expansion due to the Na<sup>+</sup> pre-intercalation, in accordance with the XRD results. Moreover, the peak at 403 cm⁻¹ represents the H<sub>2</sub>O stretching vibration, which vanishes in D-NVO<sub>x</sub>, indicating that Na⁺ substitutes the H<sub>2</sub>O molecule.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0041_R_d159978101e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Energy Environ. Sci.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks at 505 and 688 cm<sup>−1</sup> are attributed to the stretching of V3─O and V2─O, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0027_R_d159978101e935" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the peak at 988 cm<sup>−1</sup> is scribed to the stretching of V─OH<sub>2</sub>, which disappears in the cation-doped structures, suggesting that Na<sup>+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> are successfully introduced into the VO layer and substitute the water molecule (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), consistent with TGA results.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202404685-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/71c482c5-f723-4124-8845-dcb7c27d7a40/aenm202404685-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/71c482c5-f723-4124-8845-dcb7c27d7a40/aenm202404685-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/89cd7ddb-0354-4c5d-a19d-1c85265d7641/aenm202404685-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/71c482c5-f723-4124-8845-dcb7c27d7a40/aenm202404685-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202404685-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202404685" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural and chemical analysis of the VO and D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. a) EPR spectra. b) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption and desorption isotherms (the inset: the corresponding pore size distributions). c) Raman spectra. d) V 2p XPS spectra. e) O 1s XPS spectra. f) Model structure for D-NVO<sub>x</sub>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The surface chemical composition and defects in D-NVO<sub>x</sub> were further examined using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). The XPS survey spectra for D-NVO<sub>x</sub> demonstrate the characteristic peaks of Na 1s, V 2p, and O 1s (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0002">2d</a>, the V 2p peaks of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> (517.3&nbsp;eV for V 2p<sub>2/3</sub>, 524.5&nbsp;eV for V 2p<sub>2/1</sub>) are slightly shifted by 0.2&nbsp;eV compared to VO, indicating alterations in electron delocalization within the V─O lattice due to defect formation. The higher V<sup>4+</sup> content in D-NVO<sub>x</sub> (38% in D-NVO<sub>x</sub> vs 28% in VO) is primarily due to Na<sup>+</sup> doping. The O 1s XPS spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0002">2e</a>) for both D-NVO<sub>x</sub> and VO display three peaks at 530.4, 531.6, and 532.8&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the V─O, defective oxygen (O<sub>v</sub>), and ─OH group, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0042_R_d159978101e1017" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">42</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0043_R_d159978101e1017" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Energy Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, D-NVO<sub>x</sub> is rich in oxygen vacancies (25%) compared to pristine VO (16%), consistent with EPR data. Similar deficient characteristics were observed in D-KVO<sub>x</sub> as well (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, a summary of the defect concentration and textural properties of VO, D-KVO<sub>x</sub>, and D-NVO<sub>x</sub>, derived from XPS and BET analyses is provided in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). These results confirm defect formation and lattice distortion following cation insertion as illustrated in the atomic model for D-NVO<sub>x</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0002">2f</a>). The increase in V<sup>4+</sup> is associated with the formation of oxygen vacancies and structural defects, which have been proven to narrow the energy bandgap and reinforce electrical conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0043_R_d159978101e1045" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Energy Mater.">43</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0044_R_d159978101e1045" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nano Energy">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These characteristics can endow D-NVO<sub>x</sub> with rapid Na-ion diffusion kinetics and a robust structure.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202404685-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202404685-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>To evaluate the significance of the deep layer pillaring on electrochemical performance, we assembled SIBs with the as-fabricated anode, Na counter electrode, and NaClO<sub>4</sub>-based non-aqueous electrolyte. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0003">3a</a> presents the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of D-NVO<sub>x</sub>, D-KVO<sub>x</sub>, and pristine VO at a sweep rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>‒1</sup> between 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V. The VO electrode displays a multi-redox reaction curve in the first cycle, which significantly diminishes in subsequent cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). This dramatic change in voltage profiles is mainly attributed to the insertion of Na⁺ into the crystalline framework in the&nbsp;initial cycle.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0013_R_d159978101e1079" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> and D-KVO<sub>x</sub> electrodes exhibit broader CV curves and higher current intensities, suggesting a pseudocapacitive response, which is characteristic of amorphous electrode materials due to their lack of crystalline phase transitions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0048_R_d159978101e1089" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nano Energy">48</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0049_R_d159978101e1089" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Energy Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the CV curves of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> are overlapping in the subsequent cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), indicating better electrochemical reaction reversibility. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0003">3b</a> presents the initial galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) profiles of the as-prepared samples. At a current density of 0.05 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode achieves the highest initial discharge capacity of 650 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> with an impressive initial Coulombic efficiency of 84%. This result is mainly attributed to the tailored structural advantages of the D-NVO<sub>x</sub>, which outperforms both D-KVO<sub>x</sub> (600 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>) and VO (574 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>). Remarkably, the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode demonstrates outstanding cycling stability, exhibiting negligible capacity decay over 200 cycles with a capacity retention of 99% at a low current density of 0.1 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, surpassing D-KVO<sub>x</sub> (93%) and VO (87%) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0003">3c</a>). Even after 500 cycles at a higher rate of 1.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode retains the highest reversible capacity of 355 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>, which is ≈50% higher than that of VO (177 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). This result indicates that deep pillaring with appropriate cations significantly stabilizes the bulk layered phase and enhances cycling stability. Despite the superior performance, all the electrodes observed capacity loss during the initial cycling due to the formation of the solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI), which is influenced by the large surface area of the nanosheets and the increased reactivity associated with the generated oxygen vacancies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0057_R_d159978101e1139" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Adv. Mater.">57</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0058_R_d159978101e1139" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv. Mater.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202404685-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/349724c9-ee30-42c0-aec7-5d6f88931c08/aenm202404685-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/349724c9-ee30-42c0-aec7-5d6f88931c08/aenm202404685-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/59653812-d8fb-4885-b485-b4b4b4ae81e3/aenm202404685-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/349724c9-ee30-42c0-aec7-5d6f88931c08/aenm202404685-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202404685-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202404685" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical performance of VO, D-KVO<sub>x</sub>, and D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. a) Cyclic voltammetry curves at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>‒1</sup>. b) Initial galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V at 0.05 A g<sup>‒1</sup>. c) Cycle performance at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>‒1</sup>. d) Rate performance at current densities ranging from 0.05 to 5 A g<sup>‒1</sup>. e) Charge/discharge profiles of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> at different current densities. f) Capacity versus current density plot of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> with previously reported VO electrodes for SIBs with various modifications.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0011" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0011_R_d159978101e1180" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A">11</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0016" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0016_R_d159978101e1180" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0045" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0045_R_d159978101e1180" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nano Lett.">45</a>-<a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0056" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0056_R_d159978101e1180" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Nano Energy">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> g) Long-term cycling performance at 2.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>. h) Comparison plot of the electrochemical performance of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> with recent anode materials for SIBs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The rate performance of VO, D-KVO<sub>x</sub>, and D-NVO<sub>x</sub> was further evaluated from 0.05 to 5.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0003">3d</a>). Notably, all guest ion-doped anodes exhibit superior rate performance compared to pristine VO, as verified by their higher reversible capacities across the entire range of current densities. Remarkably, the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode delivers high specific capacities of 505, 441, 416, 368, 356, and 304 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at current densities of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, respectively. Simultaneously, even at a high rate of 5.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode retains a substantial capacity of 280 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>, highlighting its superior rate capability. Importantly, when the current density is reduced back to 0.05 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode promptly recovers a high reversible capacity of 430 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>, which is significantly higher than that of VO (270 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>). Furthermore, the highly overlapping GCD profiles presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0003">3e</a> strongly support the outstanding rate capability of the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode. The rate performance of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> surpasses that of state-of-the-art V-based electrodes for SIBs with various modifications (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), including both non-pillared and pillared variants (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0003">3f</a>), such as V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/C,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0045_R_d159978101e1242" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nano Lett.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> bilayered V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanobelt,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0046_R_d159978101e1252" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - ACS Nano">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> hollow nanosphere,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0011_R_d159978101e1262" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> amorphous VO<sub>x</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0047_R_d159978101e1269" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Mater. Chem. A">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A-V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/C-WCS,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0048_R_d159978101e1279" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nano Energy">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> α-VO<sub>x</sub>/V<sub>2</sub>C,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0049_R_d159978101e1289" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Energy Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> c-VS<sub>2</sub>@VOOH,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0050_R_d159978101e1296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Mater. Chem. A">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>∙nH<sub>2</sub>O-xerogel,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0050_R_d159978101e1308" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Mater. Chem. A">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ni₀.₂₅V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>•nH<sub>2</sub>O,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0051_R_d159978101e1320" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energy Storage Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CaV<sub>8</sub>O<sub>20</sub>⋅xH<sub>2</sub>O,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0052_R_d159978101e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Chem. Eng. J.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CaV<sub>4</sub>O<sub>9</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0053_R_d159978101e1341" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nat. Commun.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>@CMK-3,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0054_R_d159978101e1351" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>1.1</sub>V<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7.9</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0055_R_d159978101e1363" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Energy Storage Mater.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and HE-NVP-0.2 (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.8</sub>(CrMnFeZnAl)<sub>0.2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0056_R_d159978101e1379" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Nano Energy">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The long-term cycling stability and corresponding Coulombic efficiency of the prepared electrodes were assessed, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0003">3g</a>. The D-NVO<sub>x</sub> anode demonstrates excellent cycling stability at a high rate of 2 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, maintaining an impressive reversible capacity of 294 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> over 2000 cycles with an outstanding capacity retention of over 96%, compared to only 48% for VO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0003">3g</a>). Moreover, the Coulombic efficiency values of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> anode show no degradation and remain consistently close to 100%. Finally, the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode exhibits superior cyclic stability compared to most commonly reported SIB anodes, including those based on Bi-based, V-based, Sb-based, titanium oxide-based, graphite, hard carbon anodes, and others (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0003">3h</a>; Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), indicating excellent structural stability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202404685-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202404685-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Energy Storage Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>Ex situ characterizations were conducted to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism and structural evolution of VO and D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0004">4a</a> presents the galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles and the corresponding XRD patterns of VO. Obviously, the initial (001) peak shows a negative shift during the discharging process, suggesting the transition of the electrode from VO to Na<sub>x</sub>VO due to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>, which aligns with the bilayer structure observed in HRTEM and SAED patterns (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0004">4b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0059_R_d159978101e1436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - ACS Nano">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the charging process, the intensity of the (001) peak progressively decreases, reflecting the gradual deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> from the interlayers. The (110) peak shows a similar trend to the (001) peak; however, it ultimately splits and disappears, suggesting the formation of a biphasic domain.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0060_R_d159978101e1444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - J. Mater. Chem. A">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the (40-1), (004), and (11-3) peaks experience obvious shifts to lower angles and vanish due to volume changes during the discharging cycle. These peaks fail to recover their original positions at the final charging state, signifying irreversible changes in both interlayer and lamellar structures. Subsequently, at lower discharging voltages (0.1≤X≤0.01), as the intercalated Na<sup>+</sup> is about to be removed, a new peak emerges at 38.8° corresponding to VO<sub>2</sub>, indicating structural collapse into metastable vanadium dioxide VO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0061_R_d159978101e1455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - J. Mater. Chem.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This phase gap is induced by sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, and the clear separation between deconvoluted diffraction peaks indicates an uneven distribution of Na<sup>+</sup> within the VO structure, leading to internal strains and ultimately structural degradation (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0062_R_d159978101e1468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - ACS Energy Lett.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is worth noting that VO demonstrates a reaction mechanism similar to its bulk counterparts,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0063_R_d159978101e1474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - J. Mater. Chem. A">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> suggesting that it cannot overcome intrinsic kinetic and structural limitations.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202404685-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d372cccd-35d1-4709-9310-b60aee386ae6/aenm202404685-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d372cccd-35d1-4709-9310-b60aee386ae6/aenm202404685-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1fd7a6d5-5246-4f64-ad4f-7974b2b8c91a/aenm202404685-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d372cccd-35d1-4709-9310-b60aee386ae6/aenm202404685-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202404685-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202404685" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Energy storage mechanism of VO and D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. a) The GCD profiles at 0.1 A g<sup>‒1</sup> and corresponding Ex situ XRD patterns for selected states of VO. b) HRTEM images and relevant SEAD pattern at the fully discharged state of VO. c) The GCD profiles and corresponding Ex situ XRD patterns for selected states of D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. d) HRTEM images and relevant SEAD pattern at the fully discharged state of D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. e) Ex situ XPS spectra of V 2p of the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode at a fully discharged and charged state. f) Ex situ Na 1s XPS spectra during the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption/desorption of the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>On the other hand, the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode demonstrates a distinctly different mechanism. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0004">4c</a>, the characteristic (001) peak progressively shifts from 14.2 to 16.5 Å during the discharge process, consistent with the 2-2.3 Å value reported for the intercalation of two Na<sup>+</sup> ions in layered VO.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0059_R_d159978101e1524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - ACS Nano">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This peak returns to its initial position during subsequent charging, demonstrating the high reversibility of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> in response to Na⁺ ion insertion and extraction. More importantly, the (110) peak remains nearly constant throughout the sodiation-desodiation cycle, with no significant splitting observed across the entire voltage plateau. This indicates that the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode effectively inhibits phase transitions, maintains structural integrity, and successfully modulates the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion mechanism. Moreover, ex situ XRD investigations disclose that D-NVO<sub>x</sub> does not exhibit any new peaks even after 100 discharge cycles at a high rate of 2.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>, following a similar trend to its first cycle at a low rate (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). This remarkable stability contrasts with surface-pillared layered structures, which often experience pronounced structural changes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0020_R_d159978101e1544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Energy">20</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0023_R_d159978101e1544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0026_R_d159978101e1544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Environ. Sci.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further elucidate the structural evolution of D-NVO<sub>x</sub>, TEM, HRTEM, and SEM characterizations were performed. Ex situ TEM images reveal that Na⁺ embedding into the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode does not disrupt the morphology or structure (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The HRTEM image also shows reversible intercalation, consistent with the ex situ XRD results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0004">4d</a>). SEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information) further confirmed that the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> morphology returned to the original ultrathin nanosheets after cycling. However, the HRTEM image shows many dislocations caused by defects and disorders after sodiation process, which create additional active sites for ion storage, thereby significantly enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity.</p>
            
            <p>Ex situ XPS analysis was then performed to reveal the valence changes in D-NVO<sub>x</sub> during the charge/discharge process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0004">4e</a>). Upon the initial discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V, the intensity of V<sup>4+</sup> increases, and two V<sup>3+</sup> peaks emerge at 515.8 and 524.7&nbsp;eV, while the V<sup>5+</sup> peak intensity decreases significantly. After charging to 3.0&nbsp;V, V<sup>3+</sup> and some of V<sup>4+</sup> were oxidized back to V<sup>5+</sup>, further confirming the excellent reversibility of the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> anode. It is worth noting that the slight shift of the V2p characteristic peak during Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation can be attributed to electron redistribution among ions or atoms. Moreover, the changes in the intensity of the Na 1s characteristic peak at 1072.6&nbsp;eV further validate the reversible Na⁺ intercalation/deintercalation process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0004">4f</a>). These findings confirm that D-NVO<sub>x</sub> undergoes reversible multi-electron reactions during charge/discharge processes.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202404685-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202404685-sec-0060-title">2.4 Electrochemical Kinetics Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>To clarify the reaction kinetics beyond the superior cycling performance of D-NVO<sub>x</sub>, in situ Nyquist plots were examined at various voltage states during initial discharge and charge cycles (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0005">5a</a>). Each Nyquist plot consists of an inclined line in the low-frequency region and a semicircle in the high-frequency region, corresponding to Warburg impedance (Z<sub>w</sub>), charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>), and ohmic resistance (R<sub>Ω</sub>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0064_R_d159978101e1622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Adv. Energy Mater.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The R<sub>ct</sub> value of the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode gradually decreased during the initial discharge, approaching a minimum of 23.36 Ω at 0.01&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0005">5b</a>). This reduction is primarily attributed to oxygen vacancies accelerating Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. Upon charging to 3.0&nbsp;V, the R<sub>ct</sub> value gradually increases, peaking at 45.26 Ω (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0005">5c</a>), and then decreases again when discharged back to 0.01&nbsp;V. This regular variation in R<sub>ct</sub> values indicates the high reversibility of the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode and its faster interfacial reactions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0005">5d</a>). Notably, the R<sub>ct</sub> values during the second discharge process are nearly identical to those in the first discharge cycle, implying reduced polarization. Additionally, while the evolution of R<sub>ct</sub> reflects changes in total resistance, the contribution of R<sub>Ω</sub> is minimal and remains almost constant at 9.54 Ω (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0005">5d</a>). In comparison, the VO electrode exhibits a similar trend to D-NVO<sub>x</sub> but with a significantly higher charge transfer impedance of 151.7 Ω due to its inherently poor ionic conductivity (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode shows a much lower R<sub>ct</sub> value of 43.7&nbsp;Ω (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0005">5a</a>). This reduction is primarily due to increased oxygen vacancies and a narrowed bandgap caused by the incorporation of Na<sup>+</sup> into the deeper V─O layers, which results in accelerated reaction kinetics. To explore the kinetic properties of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> and VO during repeated cycles, ex situ EIS measurements were performed at a rate of 2.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup> in different cycles, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0005">5e</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). The R<sub>ct</sub> value of VO increases rapidly to 408.8 Ω over the first 1000 cycles, highlighting its slow kinetics and higher polarization. In contrast, the R<sub>ct</sub> value of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> exhibits a slight increase in the initial cycles and stabilizes after 100 cycles, approaching only 121&nbsp;Ω over 1000 cycles. This suggests that Na deep pillaring of VO results in faster diffusion kinetics and superior structural stability over long-term cycling.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202404685-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9b5ebcc4-9473-4632-9c79-fbff35f2b9fa/aenm202404685-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9b5ebcc4-9473-4632-9c79-fbff35f2b9fa/aenm202404685-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/57e936d9-8893-45f1-bc31-7bda64c6b7fc/aenm202404685-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9b5ebcc4-9473-4632-9c79-fbff35f2b9fa/aenm202404685-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202404685-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202404685" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical kinetics analysis of VO and D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. a) In situ Nyquist plots of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> collected at various potential states during the initial dis/charge cycles. Typical Nyquist plots were obtained at b) the first discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V and c) the first charge up to 3.0&nbsp;V. d) EIS parameters extracted from the equivalent circuits. e) R<sub>ct</sub> values of VO and D-NVO<sub>x</sub> derived from their equivalent circuits at the fully discharged state after different cycles at 2.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>. Comparison of f) sodium diffusion coefficients (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) and g) GITT profiles of VO and D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. h) CV curves of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> at various scan rates and i) its capacitive contribution ratio.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further reveal the kinetic differences between VO and D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrodes, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests and their derived Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) are illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0005">5f,g</a>. The D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values for D-NVO<sub>x</sub> range between 10⁻⁸ and 10⁻⁹ cm<sup>2</sup> s⁻¹, which is two orders of magnitude higher than those of bare VO. This improvement is attributed to its unique broad diffusion channels of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> that facilitate fast ion transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0049_R_d159978101e1752" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Energy Mater.">49</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0065_R_d159978101e1752" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, they far surpass previously reported D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values of tailored nano-engineered vanadium-oxide-based electrodes (10<sup>−</sup>¹⁰–10<sup>−</sup>¹<sup>2</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−</sup>¹), validating their rapid charge transfer kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0049_R_d159978101e1772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Energy Mater.">49</a>, <a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0065_R_d159978101e1772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, CV curves at various scan rates ranging from 0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>‒1</sup> were collected to examine the charge storage behavior of the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0005">5h</a>). The relationship between peak current <i>(I</i>, mA) and scan rate <i>(V</i>, V) can be quantitatively analyzed using the log(I) versus log(<i>V</i>) plot. The fitting results (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information) show that the <i>b</i> values for D-NVO<sub>x</sub> excel 0.85, verifying the significant role of pseudocapacitive behavior in determining the actual capacity. Simultaneously, the pseudocapacitive contribution increases from 70% to 88% with increasing scan rates, (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0005">5i</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating the fast diffusion dynamics for D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. In contrast, the capacitive contributions ratio of the VO electrode at different scan rates does not exceed 70% (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the deep layer pillaring strategy effectively boosts the kinetic performance of the battery.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202404685-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202404685-sec-0070-title">2.5 Theoretical Calculations and Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were conducted to thoroughly investigate the electronic structure of D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. Based on experimental results, we constructed three models of pristine VO, surface pillaring Na-doped VO (S-NVO), and deep pillaring Na-doped VO structure (D-NVO<sub>x</sub>) as shown in <b>Figures</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0006">6a</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information). We first confirmed the formation of disordered polyhedral structures and oxygen vacancies in the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> model.
