Compared with VO and S-NVO, D-NVO<sub>x</sub> possesses more reaction sites which could facilitate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. In addition, the discharge process of D-NVO<sub>x</sub> with different Na<sup>+</sup> embedded amounts was simulated through DFT calculations (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). The results show that the structural model maintains robust stability even in the presence of doping and defects. The band structure and density of states (DOS) calculations were performed to gain a deeper understanding of the electronic properties of VO, S-NVO, and D-NVO<sub>x</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0006">6b</a>). The bare VO possesses a significant band gap of 1.48&nbsp;eV, suggesting its semiconductor characteristics with poor electrical conductivity. This notable band gap near the Fermi energy level vanishes in all the Na-doped VO systems (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0006">6b</a>). Particularly, upon achieving a deep layer pillaring D-NVO<sub>x</sub>, the bandgap is significantly reshaped, resulting in a higher density of electronic states. This enhancement is attributed to the synergistic effect of doping, lattice disorder, and oxygen vacancies, which increases the number of unpaired electrons and improves electronic conductivity, thus boosting the reactivity of the material and promoting the adsorption of Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202404685-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202404685-bib-0030_R_d159978101e1858" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Sci.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, both electron and ion transport are greatly accelerated. This finding is further supported by the Na<sup>+</sup> binding energy (BE) analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0006">6c</a>), which shows that D-NVO<sub>x</sub> exhibits a much lower BE energy (−2.46&nbsp;eV) compared to S-NVO (−3.65&nbsp;eV) and VO (−4.79&nbsp;eV), indicating that the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> in the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> electrode is more favorable. Subsequently, the climbing image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method was implemented in the VASP toolkit to investigate the sodium diffusion barrier and its potential diffusion pathways in VO, S-NVO, and D-NVO<sub>x</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0006">6d–i</a>). In the bare VO model (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0006">6f</a>), Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion takes a more straightforward path, though it tends to skew toward one side at the centrosymmetric polyhedron due to narrow lattice spacing. This results in a significantly higher diffusion energy barrier of 0.8&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0006">6d</a>), indicating sluggish kinetics and an undesirable structure. In the case of Na-doped VO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0006">6g</a>), Na<sup>+</sup> favors traveling along a zigzag route along the b-axis from one polyhedral site to the adjacent polyhedral site, with a reduced energy barrier of 0.49&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0006">6d</a>). This reduction in the energy barrier can be attributed to the expanded lattice spacing due to Na doping, allowing for better reaction kinetics. On the other hand, in the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> model (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0006">6h,i</a>), Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffuse from one site to an asymmetrical site driven by deficient polyhedral and oxygen vacancies. This process not only widens diffusion paths but also exposes fully active sites, allowing Na<sup>+</sup> ions to move more freely with a much lower diffusion energy barrier of only 0.05&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202404685-fig-0006">6e,i</a>), suggesting its rapid kinetics and favorable structural design, resulting in excellent electrochemical performance. These findings strongly affirm that deep layer pillaring modulates the electronic structure of pristine VO and provides more efficient ion-migration paths for rapid and stable electrode kinetics.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202404685-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/399a8ea2-56e8-4f29-9acf-1222a0f4561c/aenm202404685-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/399a8ea2-56e8-4f29-9acf-1222a0f4561c/aenm202404685-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/890cd034-ca43-443c-99c4-ff556ee77424/aenm202404685-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/399a8ea2-56e8-4f29-9acf-1222a0f4561c/aenm202404685-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202404685-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202404685" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Theoretical calculations for the VO-base electrode. a) Model structure of the inserted Na atom in D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. b) Total and partial DOS of VO, S-NVO, and D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. c) The calculated binding energies of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the VO, S-NVO, and D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. d) Diffusion energy barrier diagrams for VO, S-NVO, and D-NVO<sub>x</sub>, and e) detailed view through the deficient disordered D-NVO<sub>x</sub>. Potential migration paths for Na<sup>+</sup> in f) VO, g) S-NVO, h) D-NVO<sub>x</sub>, and i) side view of D-NVO<sub>x</sub>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202404685-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202404685-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, we propose an effective synergistic strategy to regulate the electronic structure of vanadium oxide, enabling reversible and rapid pseudocapacitive multi-electron reactions. This strategy involves the synthesis of novel Na-doped layered VO (D-NVO<sub>x</sub>) electrodes through a straightforward low-temperature hydrothermal method, resulting in significant structural modifications such as enlarged lattice spacing, oxygen vacancies, and segmentally disordered V─O layers. These modifications suggest that Na<sup>+</sup> cations are incorporated into the deep VO layers, imparting unique properties not observed in pristine VO or surface/shallow-pillared layered metal oxides. Based on comprehensive experimental measurements and DFT calculations reveal that this synergistic modulation through deep-layer Na⁺ pillaring effectively suppresses phase transitions induces reversible Na⁺ pseudocapacitive intercalation, and reconstructs low-energy barrier channels, achieving exceptional electron and Na⁺ transport. Simultaneously, the oxygen vacancies dramatically enhance electronic conductivity and reinforce structural stability. The reduced crystallinity and lattice distortion generate numerous nanointerfaces, providing additional active sites for fast surface Na-ion storage. Owing to its structural superiorities, the D-NVO<sub>x</sub> anode demonstrates a high reversible specific capacity (505&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>‒1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>‒1</sup>), outstanding long-term cyclability (96% capacity retention over 2000 cycles at 2.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>), and extraordinary rate capability (maintaining a high capacity of 280 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 5.0 A g<sup>‒1</sup>), surpassing most transition metal oxide electrodes. This study offers a viable strategy to address intrinsic limitations in kinetics and reaction reversibility of layered oxide materials, paving the way for the development of superior anodes for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
